10.1016/j.jmp.2021.102580
The current discussions relating to the replication crisis and the untrustworthiness of empirical evidence have revealed several crucial misapprehensions relating to the frequentist approach to statistical modeling and inference. The discussion that follows will focus primarily on certain questionable statistical practices in psychology, which include: (a) the use/abuse of estimation-based effect sizes and observed Confidence Intervals (CIs), (b) the (mis)interpretation of error probabilities as conditional on hypotheses giving rise to  
                         
                            P 
                            
                               ( 
                               D 
                               ∣ 
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                            ≠ 
                            P 
                            
                               ( 
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                               ∣ 
                               D 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , and (c) the use of noncentrality-based CIs for effect sizes, such as Cohen’s  
                         d 
                      . Shedding light on these misconceptions is of paramount importance because they contribute significantly to the Tower of Babel-type exchanges about replicability and the trustworthiness of empirical evidence, undermining their cogency and suggesting misleading ‘solutions’.

The different perspectives on statistical practice from different disciplines bring to the table several additional confusions to the current discussions on replication. A prime example of that is the Null Hypothesis Significance Testing (NHST) practice in psychology that is often erroneously conflated with proper frequentist testing; see  Nickerson (2000) . A closer look at its details reveals that the NHST constitutes an oversimplified caricature of frequentist testing, which can only be described as a misrepresentation permeated with fundamental confusions; see  Spanos (2019) . In what follows, some of these confusions are interrogated with a view to counteract the strategy of using NHST as a strawman to make a case against frequentist testing, and then use its alleged flaws to justify half-baked reforms and recommendations for statistical practice; see  Haig (2016, 2020)  and  Mayo (2018) .

It is argued that when (a)–(c) are viewed in the context of model-based frequentist inference, which revolves around the concept of a prespecified parametric model

                               x

A case is made that more reliable information about the magnitude of the ‘scientific effect’ is provided by a testing-based effect size (warranted discrepancy) stemming from the post-data severity evaluation of testing results. Also, the construction of noncentrality-based CIs for effect sizes, such as Cohen’s

                      , is permeated by several obfuscations, including (i) the confusion between  factual  (point and interval estimation) and  hypothetical  (testing)  reasoning , and (ii) their construction casts aside of the duality between CIs and N–P testing. It is shown how such an CIs for Cohen’s

Probability theory provides the foundation and overarching framework for statistical modeling and inference, and the probabilistic concepts used need to be defined mathematically precise form to avoid confusion and misinterpretations. As a prelude to the discussion of the main issues (a)–(c) mentioned above, this section summarizes the basic results of frequentist inference in precise mathematical notation and highlights several confusions and misapprehensions to be avoided.

Modern model-based frequentist inference, as recast by  Fisher (1922, 1925) , revolves around a prespecified  parametric statistical model , generically defined by:

                         . The effectiveness of a CI is evaluated in terms of the sampling distribution of a pivotal quantity and calibrated using the coverage probability. Similarly, the effectiveness of a Neyman–Pearson (N–P) test is evaluated in terms of the sampling distribution of a test statistic, with its optimality calibrated in terms of the relevant error probabilities (the significance level and the power). These error probabilities, however, presuppose [i]

Hypothesis testing poses questions whether a hypothetical null value, say  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                            , is ‘close enough’ to  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                             in the sense that the difference  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           ∗ 
                                        
                                     
                                     − 
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           0 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                             is ‘statistically negligible’; a concept operationalized using error probabilities. A hypothesis or an inferential claim being ‘exactly correct’ ( 
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                                  = 
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        ∗ 
                                     
                                  
                               
                            ) has no place in statistics, and in frequentist inference, we can learn from data about  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                             without discovering its true value, unless by happenstance.

The underlying logic of frequentist testing is based on hypothetical reasoning as it relates to different prespecified values of  
                               θ 
                             that could ‘approximate closely’  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            , in the sense that the difference  
                               
                                  ‖ 
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        ∗ 
                                     
                                  
                                  − 
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                                  ‖ 
                               
                            , where  
                               
                                  ‖ 
                                  . 
                                  ‖ 
                               
                             denotes a distance function (norm), is ‘statistically insignificant (negligible)’, or ‘statistically significant (substantial)’. The primary role of the relevant error probabilities is to operationalize the concepts of ‘statistically significant/insignificant’. The effectiveness of a N–P test  
                               
                                  
                                     T 
                                  
                                  
                                     α

                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                            . The test statistic  
                               
                                  d

                                     X

                                  ‖

                                  − 
                                  
                                     
                                        θ 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                                  ‖ 
                               
                            , in the sense that (i) it is framed using the best estimator for  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            , and (ii) it increases monotonically with this distance. For instance, the test

                                  τ

Pre-data error probabilities calibrate the  capacity  of a frequentist procedure to give rise to learning from data about  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            . Estimation and testing pose distinct questions to the data and elicit different answers, but hypothetical reasoning provides a more powerful tool in interrogating the data, enabling testing to generate more informative answers about  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            .

It is very important to emphasize that  Neyman and Pearson (1933)  proposed two crucial preconditions for the effectiveness of N–P testing which relate to the framing of  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                             and  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                            :

(i) they constitute a partition of  
                               Θ 
                            , in a way that renders

(ii) the type I error probability the more serious of the two; see also  Neyman (1952) .

The partition of  
                               Θ 
                             is crucial in light of the primary objective of frequentist inference since the ‘true’ value  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                             might lie outside the union of  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                             and  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                            , turning N–P testing into a wild goose chase. For instance, in the case of the simple Normal model in  (1)  it makes no sense to frame  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                             and  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                               
                             as two simple hypotheses ( Berger & Wolpert, 1988 ):  
                               
                                  (18) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 
                                                    H 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    0 
                                                 
                                              
                                              :  
                                              μ 
                                              = 
                                              1

                                              :  
                                              μ 
                                              = 
                                              − 
                                              1 
                                              , 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            by invoking the Neyman–Pearson (N–P) lemma. This is because the N–P lemma  assumes  a partition  
                               
                                  Θ 
                                  ≔ 
                                  
                                     { 
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           0 
                                        
                                     
                                     , 
                                     
                                        
                                           θ 
                                        
                                        
                                           1 
                                        
                                     
                                     } 
                                  
                               
                            , and the framing in  (18)  runs afoul both preconditions (i)–(ii); see  Spanos (2011) .

Precondition (ii) ensures that the power of the test – its capacity to detect discrepancies from  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                             – is defined over the relevant range, i.e. the potential range of values of  
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            . Hence, when no such reliable information is available, the N–P test will be be more effective (reliable and precise) by using a two-sided partition of  
                               
                                  Θ 
                                  = 
                                  R 
                               
                            , say:  
                               
                                  (19) 
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           
                                              
                                                 
                                                    H

                                                    1

                                              μ 
                                              ≠ 
                                              
                                                 
                                                    μ 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    0 
                                                 
                                              
                                              .

In cases where such reliable information is available, as in the case of example 4 in Section  5 , the N–P test will be more effective when using a directional framing for  
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0

                                     ∗

                            .

A key confusion in NHST that permeates current discussions about replication is that the  
                            p 
                         -value, the type I and II error probabilities as well as the power are conditional probabilities; they are not. For instance, in a highly influential paper Cohen (1974) argues:  “When one tests 
                         
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         
                         , one is finding the probability that the data ( 
                         
                            D 
                         
                         ) could have arisen if 
                         
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         
                         were true, 
                         
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  D 
                                  | 
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         
                         .If that probability is small, then it can be concluded that if 
                         
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         
                         is true, then 
                         
                            D 
                         
                         is unlikely. Now, what really is at issue, what is always the real issue, is the probability that 
                         
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         
                         is true, given the data, 
                         
                            
                               P 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        H 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                                  | 
                                  D 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         
                         , the inverse probability.”(p. 998)

Taking that claim at face value, numerous papers in the replication literature use the claim:  
                            
                               (29) 
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           P 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              D 
                                              ∣ 
                                              
                                                 
                                                    H 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    0 
                                                 
                                              
                                              )

                                           P 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              
                                                 
                                                    H 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    0 
                                                 
                                              
                                              ∣ 
                                              D 
                                              ) 
                                           
                                           , 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         to admonish frequentist testing; see  Nickerson (2000) . A quick google search of ‘p-value and prosecutor’s fallacy’ brings up more than 2,960,000 entries, even though  (29)  has nothing to do with frequentist error probabilities, or so it is argued.

Viewed in the context of frequentist model-based inference,  (29)  is mathematically an erroneous probabilistic statement. For two simple events

The misinterpretation of error probabilities as being conditional provides an example of how the informality of specifying  (29)  without any quantifiers can give rise to serious confusions. Another such confusion is the widely-accepted, but false, analogy between type I and II error probabilities and the  false positive  and  negative rates  for medical devices and test procedures which are based on conditional probabilities; see  Spanos (2010a) . This false analogy undermines the case made by a widely influential paper by  Ioannidis (2005) ; see the  Appendix .

The small  
                         
                            n 
                          
                         problem . There is always a ‘small enough’  
                            n 
                         ,  
                            
                               1 
                               ≤

                               < 
                               ∞

The potential pernicious effects of the large/small  
                            n 
                          problems on hypothesis testing have been framed in terms of two fallacies by  Mayo and Spanos (2006) .

The  fallacy of rejection : evidence  against  
                         
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          is (mis)interpreted as evidence  for  the specific alternative  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                          considered in the framing of the hypotheses.

The  fallacy of acceptance : no evidence against  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          is (mis)interpreted as evidence  for  it. This can arise when the test has very low power to detect substantively large discrepancies from  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         . The reliability of both, frequentist testing and CIs (see  (32) ), is equally vulnerable to these fallacies; see  Mayo and Spanos (2011) .

As argued in Section  2.2 , the reliability and effectiveness of inference (estimation, testing) presuppose [i]  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     M 
                                  
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  x 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          is statistically adequate, and [ii]  
                            n 
                          is large enough for the inference procedure to have adequate capacity to shed sufficient light on the substantive questions of interest. This implies that a small  
                            n 
                          will also affect the trustworthiness of ensuing evidence since it will render M–S testing ineffectual. For instance, securing the statistical adequacy of statistical models 1, 2 and 4 will require  
                            
                               n 
                               ≥ 
                               40 
                            
                          for validating the IID assumptions for two reasons. The first is that departures from IID assumptions, in the form of irregular cycles (dependence) and trends in the mean and variance (heterogeneity), require a large enough  
                            n 
                          to recur (unfold) several times to establish chance regularity patterns; see  Spanos (2019) . The second is that M–S tests require a large enough  
                            n 
                          to have sufficient power to detect departures from the model assumptions.

Lehmann (1993)  makes a strong case that there is no real incompatibility between Fisher’s view of significance testing and the Neyman–Pearson (N–P) perspective; see also  Spanos (2006b)  in the relationship between Fisher significance testing and the N–P testing.  Gigerenzer (1993)  disagrees and argues that, not only the two are incompatible, but any attempt to blend them would result in an  inconsistent hybrid  “burdened with conceptual confusion” (p. 323). The case for the alleged incompatibility is based on various pre-data renderings of the p-value. The first is the traditional recasting of the accept/reject  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          rules in terms of:

                                           p

                                                    0 
                                                 
                                              
                                              ) 
                                           
                                           > 
                                           α 
                                           , 
                                           
                                            accept  
                                           
                                              
                                                 H 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                           
                                           , 
                                        
                                        
                                           [ii] 
                                           ∗ 
                                            if  
                                           p 
                                           
                                              ( 
                                              
                                                 
                                                    x 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    0 
                                                 
                                              
                                              ) 
                                           
                                           ≤ 
                                           α 
                                           , 
                                           
                                            reject  
                                           
                                              
                                                 H 
                                              
                                              
                                                 0 
                                              
                                           
                                           . 
                                        
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                         The second is the view that the  
                            p 
                         -value denotes  the smallest significance level  
                         
                            
                               
                                  α 
                               
                               
                                  m

                                  n

                                  x

                         U

Despite their key roles in frequentist testing, the common denominator of these three renderings is a pre-data oriented interpretation ascribed to the p-value, being defined: ‘as the probability of obtaining a result ‘equal to or more extreme’ than the one observed  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                         , when  
                            
                               
                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  0

                                  H 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                         , which entangles the  
                            p 
                         -value with the particular N–P framing of

In light of the above discussion, one can argue that the alleged ‘incompatibility’ stems from conflating pre-data [before  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          is known] with post-data [after  
                            
                               τ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  
                                     
                                        x 
                                     
                                     
                                        0 
                                     
                                  
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          is known] error probabilities, and their complimentary roles in frequentist statistics; see  Spanos (2019) . The dual role of frequentist probability is articulated by  Mayo (2018)  in the form of  probativism : probability is used to assess the ‘probing capacity’ of inference procedures,  pre-data  , as well as to provide an evidential interpretation of testing results,  post-data  ( 
                            p 
                         -value, severity); see Section  5 .

What happens to inference when any of the probabilistic assumptions comprising

That is,  statistical misspecification  can be a major source of untrustworthiness since both  
                            
                               f 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  x

It is important to emphasize that all forms of statistical inference, including Bayesian, nonparametric as well as Akaike-type model selection procedures, are equally vulnerable to statistical misspecification because they impose (implicitly or explicitly) probabilistic assumptions on data  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          that need to be validated; see  Spanos (2010b) . Also, a misspecified

                                     θ 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  x 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                          renders impossible any discussion of power, significant/insignificant results, p-hacking, multiple testing, and post-data severity evaluations, since the nominal error probabilities are likely to be different from the actual ones; see  Spanos and McGuirk (2001) .

The estimation-based effect sizes were introduced ( Cohen, 1988 ) partly as a way to circumvent the large  
                         n 
                       problem. As  Abelson (1995) , p. 46, argues:  “One advantage of the raw size effect as a measure is that its expected value is independent of the size of the sample used to perform the significance test. ... On the other hand, it could be argued that with a smallish sample, one might obtain a big apparent effect without being able to reject the null hypothesis. In other words, an effect size ought not to be judged in totally in isolation, but in conjunction with the p-value.”

What about asymptotic properties of an estimator

                                  X

                            n 
                         ”. That is, asymptotic properties  cannot  ensure the reliability of inference in the sense that the actual error probabilities (types I, II, coverage) approximate closely the nominal (assumed) ones for any particular  
                            
                               
                                  z 
                               
                               
                                  0

Finally, it is crucial to re-iterate that point estimators, estimation-based effect sizes, as well as observed CIs, are highly vulnerable to statistical misspecification.

This suggests that, despite the keen support for replication ( Nosek & Lakens, 2014 ), conclusions along the lines that ‘for a particular published study (i) the statistical significance is replicated on the basis of observed CIs, but (ii) the effect size

Although noncentrality-based CIs are used for many different effect sizes ( Steiger & Fouladi, 1997 ), the discussion focuses on one typical example, Cohen’s  
                         d 
                      .

The importance of noncentrality-based CIs for Cohen’s  
                            d 
                          using the noncentral t distribution is explained in  Cumming (2012) , pp. 276–7, by employing the didactic device of a fairy tale between two groups of statistical practitioners, the grumpy Nulls and the young Alts. Using their smarts, as the tale goes, the Alts ‘discovered’ that beyond the dull old central t distribution, used exclusively by Nulls adhering to the NHST procedure, there is the neglected noncentral t distribution. The cool Alts put their ‘discovery’ to good use by framing better CIs for estimation-based effect sizes: “...  if you use Cohen’s 
                         
                            d 
                         
                         you need to use noncentral t to calculate a CI for your 
                         
                            d 
                         
                         value. ”  (p. 277). Cumming goes on to complain that: “ The beautiful, intriguing, and useful noncentral t distribution was rarely taught to students, and remained little known among researchers. It is one of the secrets of Significance Land, yet to be fully appreciated. ” (p. 277) . Far from being ‘discovered’ by the smart Alts in the 1980s ( Hedges, 1981 ), the noncentral t distribution was initially derived by  Fisher (1931)  and has been used extensively since then in a variety of contexts, including the evaluation of the power of t-tests; see  Johnson and Welch (1940) . Worse, constructing a CI for

The most serious weakness of noncentrality-based CIs is that their effectiveness is periled by discarding the duality between optimal CIs and optimal N–P tests in favor of relying on numerical simulations to compare such intervals with traditional ones in terms of the observed relative frequencies of coverage. This simulation-based numerical comparison is no substitute for minimizing the expected length of a CI because the latter is analytical in nature, not numerical, and is framed at the level of a generic, well-defined (regular) statistical model and its parameterization, irrespective of the values of the unknown parameters, as well as the sample size  
                            n 
                         .

Simulation is useful for illustrating the optimality of inferential procedures but not for replacing the optimality for inferential procedures, including point estimation, CIs and hypothesis testing. The reason is that any numerical simulation will unavoidably be highly specific with respect to (a) a particular statistical model, with (b) a particular set of prespecified values of all its parameters, (c) a particular sample size  
                            n 
                         , (d) a particular number  
                            N 
                          of replications used, as well as (d) the accuracy of the pseudorandom number algorithm employed. This renders any comparison of the effectiveness of different procedures using simulation highly vulnerable to  simulation data-dredging  that describes the practice of simulating thousands of replications by changing (i) the sample sizes  
                            n 
                         , (ii) the prespecified values for the parameters, (iii) the values of the ‘seed’ of the algorithm, as well as (iv) the number of replications  
                            N 
                         , in search of a desired result! Note that there is an infinity of choices in each of (i)–(iv). That is, numerical simulations are always local and of limited scope, instead of global and analytical, and applicable to any generic but well-defined statistical model and its unknown parameterization.

Also, numerical simulations differ from actual statistical modeling in at least one crucial respect: a good simulation design ensures that the probabilistic assumptions, such as NIID, are valid by construction, but these assumptions need to be tested and their validity secured in the case of actual data.

This brings up the last and most important weakness specific to noncentrality-based CIs. The evaluations of the reliability of different CIs based on numerical simulations utilize information that is  not  available when modeling with actual data, the prespecified value of the parameter of interest  
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                          is known. The noncentrality-based CIs require knowing the range of values of the noncentrality parameter, that involves the prespecified value of  
                            θ 
                         ; see  Algina and Keselman (2003) ,  Viechtbauer (2007) . Inevitably, when such information is used to construct noncentrality-based CIs will nullify any comparison with traditional CIs that do not require or rely on such information.

                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  ∗

                                     )

                                     X 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               
                            
                          relates to the whole interval, and not to individual points in the interval. Worse, the same literature emphasizes point estimates and observed CIs at the expense of hypothesis testing. Contrary to such views, a more reliable answer to evaluating the ‘scientific effect’ is provided by the post-data severity evaluation of testing results, discussed next.

Error statistics refines and extends the Fisher–Neyman– Pearson (F–N–P) approach to frequentist inference in an attempt to address several foundational problems. The refinement comes in the form of separating the  modeling  from the  inference facet , where the former includes  estimation ,  misspecification testing , and  respecification  in order to secure the statistical adequacy of  
                         
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               x 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , before the substantive questions of interest are posed to the data (inference); see  Mayo and Spanos (2004) ,  Spanos (2018) . Error statistics extends the F–N–P approach by distinguishing between  pre-data , and  post-data  facets of frequentist testing with a view to provide a sound evidential interpretation of the testing results by supplementing the original framing with a post-data severity evaluation; see  Mayo (1996)  and  Mayo and Spanos (2006, 2011) .

A hypothesis  
                            H 
                          passes a  severe test

                                  α

                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0

                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          accords with  
                            H 
                         , and ( S-2 ) with very high probability, test  
                            
                               
                                  T 
                               
                               
                                  α 
                               
                            
                          would have produced a result that accords less well with  
                            H 
                          than  
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  0 
                               
                            
                          does if  
                            H 
                          were false. An inferential claim  
                            H 
                          is warranted when the different ways it can be false have been adequately probed and forfended; see  Mayo (2018)  and  Mayo and Spanos (2006) .

The above discussion has brought out and shed light on several misconceptions relating to the proper implementation and interpretation of frequentist inference in general, and hypothesis testing and CIs in particular, focusing on the misleading exposition of “New Statistics” ( Cumming, 2012 ). Their importance stems from the fact that their misleading implications becloud the current discussions on replicability; see  Haig (2020) .

The conclusions from the above discussion can be summarized in the form of several key points and recommendations.

(i) The validity of the probabilistic assumptions imposed on data  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       comprising the assumed (implicitly or explicitly) statistical model  
                         
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               x 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       should be tested before drawing any inferences since statistical misspecification is likely to derail the reliability of inference and the trustworthiness of the ensuing evidence.

(ii) The underlying reasoning of frequentist inference is twofold,  factual  
                      
                         
                            
                               θ 
                               = 
                               
                                  
                                     θ 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            
                         
                       for estimation (point and interval), and  hypothetical  (‘ 
                         
                            
                               H 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       or  
                         
                            
                               H 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       is true’) for testing. Neither frequentist form of reasoning involves conditioning since the relevant scenarios are framed in terms of  
                         θ 
                       which are unobservable constants, and not events or random variables one can condition upon

(iii) Following from (ii), the interpretation of error probabilities as being conditional on ‘ 
                         
                            
                               H 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       or  
                         
                            
                               H 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                       is true’, as well as the charge that they conflate  
                         
                            P 
                            
                               ( 
                               D 
                               ∣ 
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                            
                         
                      with  
                         
                            P 
                            
                               ( 
                               
                                  
                                     H 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                               ∣ 
                               D 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                      , are completely unwarranted.

(iv) Following from (ii)–(iii), the maligning of the  
                         p 
                      -value in the discussions on replication stems in part from misapprehensions relating to pre-data and post-data error probabilities. The argument for CIs and against p-values is unwarranted since both are equally vulnerable to the large  
                         n 
                       problem, and one cannot assign probability or likelihood to an observed CI post-data.

(v) Following from (ii)–(iv), the alleged analogy between type I and II error probabilities and the false positive and negative rates is a fabrication based on misconstruing error probabilities. The latter are assigned to the testing procedure itself, calibrating its effectiveness, but the false positive/negative rates are point estimates of probabilities stemming from  conditioning  on observable Bernoulli (zero/one) events.

(vi) Following from (v), the PPV measure stems from false analogical reasoning, and constitutes a highly misinforming basis for evaluating the trustworthiness of empirical evidence.

(vii) Estimation-based effects sizes, including Cohen’s  
                         d 
                      , provide untrustworthy evidence for the magnitude of the ‘scientific effect’ because they invoke the unwarranted inferential claim  
                         
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  ̂ 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     0 
                                  
                               
                               ) 
                            
                            ≃ 
                            
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                               
                                  ∗ 
                               
                            
                         
                       for  
                         n 
                       large enough.

(viii) Optimal CIs alleviate, to some extent, the unreliability of inference problem in (iv), but that does not extend to observed CIs over which no coverage probability or likelihood can be legitimately assigned.

(ix) Noncentrality-based CIs are questionable because, in addition to ignoring (i)–(v), they ignore the optimal properties of CIs stemming from the duality with N–P testing. Such problems do not arise when optimal CIs for Cohen’s  
                         d 
                       are used.

(x) Testing-based effect sizes stemming from the post-data severity evaluation of testing results in the form of the warranted discrepancy  
                         γ 
                       from  
                         
                            
                               H 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       provide trustworthy evidence about the magnitude of the ‘scientific effect’.

(xi) The post-data severity evaluation can also be used to address several criticisms and conundrums bedeviling frequentist testing since the 1930s, including the large  
                         n 
                       problem, the use/abuse of p-values and observed CIs, the arbitrariness of choosing  
                         α 
                      , and the different formulations of  
                         
                            
                               H 
                            
                            
                               0 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               H

This formula has been used by  Ioannidis (2005)  to make a case that “ ...most published research findings are false ”, by arguing that p-hacking, multiple testing, cherry picking, and low power studies are the leading contributors by way of  (50)  to untrustworthy empirical evidence. How does  Ioannidis (2005)  make his case?

Not by reproducing the results of highly cited published empirical papers and demonstrating that their bad practices give rise to untrustworthy evidence. Closer scrutiny of  (50)  reveals that  Ioannidis (2005)  whole case is built on a  false analogy  between the type I and II  error probabilities  and the point estimation-based  false positive/negative rates . This false analogy stems from the fact that medical devices and procedures are calibrated using a large number of medical tests, say  
                         
                            20000 
                         
                      , carried out with specimens prepared in a lab that are  known  to be positive or negative. This knowledge renders  
                         F 
                       and  
                         
                            
                               F 
                            
                            ¯

                            Pr

                                     R 
                                  
                                  ¯

                               F 
                               )

                            Pr

                               R 
                               ∣ 
                               
                                  
                                     F 
                                  
                                  ¯ 
                               
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       using the corresponding observed relative frequencies based on frequencies from 2 × 2 contingency tables; see  Greenhouse and Mantel (1950) . In this sense medical diagnostic screening rates, such as the sensitivity, the specificity, as well as the PPV, represent point estimates of conditional probabilities associated with 2 × 2 contingency, and have nothing to do with frequentist testing error probabilities. Worse, these rates are vulnerable to the same criticism as all point estimates in the sense that they invoke the unwarranted inferential claim

                       large enough.

In conclusion, it is important to emphasize that questioning the cogency of the PPV does not call into question the contribution of cherry picking, data-dredging, multiple testing and p-hacking to the untrustworthiness of evidence problem; see  Mayo (2018) . Such ‘bad’ practices, however, should be considered at the level of individual empirical studies, and viewed in conjunction with other crucial contributors, the most crucial being  statistical inadequacy . When  
                         
                            
                               
                                  M 
                               
                               
                                  θ 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               x 
                               ) 
                            
                         
                       is statistically misspecified, the actual and nominal error probabilities are very different (see scenario 2 in  Table 2 ), and thus any attempt to adjust the relevant error probabilities for p-hacking and multiple testing will be futile; adjusting actual error probabilities (mistakenly) thinking they are equal to the nominal ones.
